<p><b>Myanmar</b>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[a]</a></sup> officially the <b>Republic of the Union of Myanmar</b>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[b]</a></sup> formerly known as <b>Burma</b>, is a country in <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>. It is the largest country in <a href="/wiki/Mainland_Southeast_Asia" title="Mainland Southeast Asia">Mainland Southeast Asia</a>, and has a population of about 54 million as of 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-worldometers.info_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-worldometers.info-12">[10]</a></sup> Myanmar is bordered by <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a> and <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> to its northwest, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> to its northeast, <a href="/wiki/Laos" title="Laos">Laos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a> to its east and southeast, and the <a href="/wiki/Andaman_Sea" title="Andaman Sea">Andaman Sea</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Bengal" title="Bay of Bengal">Bay of Bengal</a> to its south and southwest. The country's capital city is <a href="/wiki/Naypyidaw" title="Naypyidaw">Naypyidaw</a>, and its largest city is <a href="/wiki/Yangon" title="Yangon">Yangon</a> (Rangoon).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World_Factbook_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-World_Factbook-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Early civilisations in the area included the <a href="/wiki/Tibeto-Burman_languages" title="Tibeto-Burman languages">Tibeto-Burman</a>-speaking <a href="/wiki/Pyu_city-states" title="Pyu city-states">Pyu city-states</a> in <a href="/wiki/Upper_Myanmar" title="Upper Myanmar">Upper Myanmar</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mon_kingdoms" title="Mon kingdoms">Mon kingdoms</a> in <a href="/wiki/Lower_Myanmar" title="Lower Myanmar">Lower Myanmar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[11]</a></sup> In the 9th century, the <a href="/wiki/Bamar_people" title="Bamar people">Bamar people</a> entered the upper <a href="/wiki/Irrawaddy_River" title="Irrawaddy River">Irrawaddy</a> valley, and following the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Pagan_Kingdom" title="Pagan Kingdom">Pagan Kingdom</a> in the 1050s, the <a href="/wiki/Burmese_language" title="Burmese language">Burmese language</a>, <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Myanmar" title="Culture of Myanmar">culture</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Theravada" title="Theravada">Theravada</a> <a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Myanmar" title="Buddhism in Myanmar">Buddhism</a> slowly became dominant in the country. The <a href="/wiki/Pagan_Kingdom" title="Pagan Kingdom">Pagan Kingdom</a> fell to <a href="/wiki/First_Mongol_invasion_of_Burma" title="First Mongol invasion of Burma">Mongol invasions</a>, and several warring states emerged. In the 16th century, reunified by the <a href="/wiki/Toungoo_dynasty" title="Toungoo dynasty">Taungoo dynasty</a>, the country became the largest empire in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Southeast_Asia" title="History of Southeast Asia">history of Southeast Asia</a> for a short period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>The early 19th-century <a href="/wiki/Konbaung_dynasty" title="Konbaung dynasty">Konbaung dynasty</a> ruled over an area that included modern Myanmar and briefly controlled <a href="/wiki/Manipur" title="Manipur">Manipur</a> and <a href="/wiki/Assam" title="Assam">Assam</a> as well. The British <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a> seized control of the administration of Myanmar after three <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Burmese_Wars" title="Anglo-Burmese Wars">Anglo-Burmese Wars</a> in the 19th century, and the country became a <a href="/wiki/British_rule_in_Burma" title="British rule in Burma">British colony</a>. After a brief <a href="/wiki/Japanese_occupation_of_Burma" title="Japanese occupation of Burma">Japanese occupation</a>, Myanmar was reconquered by the Allies and <a href="/wiki/Independence_Day_(Myanmar)" title="Independence Day (Myanmar)">gained independence</a> in 1948. Following a <a href="/wiki/1962_Burmese_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1962 Burmese coup d'Ã©tat">coup d'Ã©tat in 1962</a>, it became a <a href="/wiki/Military_dictatorship" title="Military dictatorship">military dictatorship</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Burma_Socialist_Programme_Party" title="Burma Socialist Programme Party">Burma Socialist Programme Party</a>.
</p><p>For most of its independent years, the country has been engulfed in rampant ethnic strife and <a href="/wiki/List_of_ethnic_groups_in_Myanmar" title="List of ethnic groups in Myanmar">its myriad ethnic groups</a> have been involved in one of the world's <a href="/wiki/Internal_conflict_in_Myanmar" title="Internal conflict in Myanmar">longest-running ongoing civil wars</a>. During this time, the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> and several other organisations have reported consistent and systemic <a href="/wiki/Human_rights_in_Myanmar" title="Human rights in Myanmar">human rights</a> violations in the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNHR_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNHR-15">[13]</a></sup> In 2011, the <a href="/wiki/State_Peace_and_Development_Council" title="State Peace and Development Council">military junta</a> was officially dissolved following a <a href="/wiki/2010_Myanmar_general_election" title="2010 Myanmar general election">2010 general election</a>, and a nominally civilian government was installed. This, along with the release of <a href="/wiki/Aung_San_Suu_Kyi" title="Aung San Suu Kyi">Aung San Suu Kyi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Political_prisoner" title="Political prisoner">political prisoners</a> and successful <a href="/wiki/2015_Myanmar_general_election" title="2015 Myanmar general election">elections in 2015</a>, improved the country's human rights record and foreign relations and led to the easing of trade and other <a href="/wiki/Economic_sanctions" title="Economic sanctions">economic sanctions</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Easing_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Easing-16">[14]</a></sup> although the country's treatment of its ethnic minorities, particularly in connection with the <a href="/wiki/Rohingya_conflict" title="Rohingya conflict">Rohingya conflict</a>, continued to be condemned by international organizations and many nations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MinorityTreatment_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-MinorityTreatment-17">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following the <a href="/wiki/2020_Myanmar_general_election" title="2020 Myanmar general election">2020 Myanmar general election</a>, in which <a href="/wiki/Aung_San_Suu_Kyi" title="Aung San Suu Kyi">Aung San Suu Kyi</a>'s party won a clear majority in both houses, the <a href="/wiki/Tatmadaw" title="Tatmadaw">Burmese military (Tatmadaw)</a> again seized power <a href="/wiki/2021_Myanmar_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="2021 Myanmar coup d'Ã©tat">in a coup d'Ã©tat</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-18">[16]</a></sup> The coup, which was widely condemned, led to <a href="/wiki/2021%E2%80%932022_Myanmar_protests" title="2021â2022 Myanmar protests">continuous ongoing widespread protests in Myanmar</a> and has been marked by violent <a href="/wiki/Political_repression" title="Political repression">political repression</a> by the military.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[17]</a></sup> The military also arrested <a href="/wiki/Aung_San_Suu_Kyi" title="Aung San Suu Kyi">Aung San Suu Kyi</a> and charged her with crimes ranging from corruption to the violation of COVID-19 protocols, all of which have been labeled as "politically motivated" by independent observers, in order to remove her from public life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>Myanmar is a member of the <a href="/wiki/East_Asia_Summit" title="East Asia Summit">East Asia Summit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Non-Aligned_Movement" title="Non-Aligned Movement">Non-Aligned Movement</a>, <a href="/wiki/ASEAN" title="ASEAN">ASEAN</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Bengal_Initiative_for_Multi-Sectoral_Technical_and_Economic_Cooperation" title="Bay of Bengal Initiative for Multi-Sectoral Technical and Economic Cooperation">BIMSTEC</a>, but it is not a member of the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a> despite once being part of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>. The country is very rich in <a href="/wiki/Natural_resource" title="Natural resource">natural resources</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Jade" title="Jade">jade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gemstone" title="Gemstone">gems</a>, <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">oil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Teak_in_Myanmar" title="Teak in Myanmar">teak</a> and other minerals, as well as also endowed with <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a>, having the highest <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">solar power</a> potential compared to other countries of the Great <a href="/wiki/Mekong" title="Mekong">Mekong</a> Subregion. However, Myanmar has long suffered from instability, factional violence, corruption, poor infrastructure, as well as a long history of colonial exploitation with little regard to human development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[19]</a></sup> In 2013, its GDP (nominal) stood at US$56.7Â billion and its GDP (<a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power_parity" title="Purchasing power parity">PPP</a>) at US$221.5Â billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-imf2_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-imf2-22">[20]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Economic_inequality" title="Economic inequality">income gap</a> in Myanmar is among the widest in the world, as a large proportion of the economy is controlled by <a href="/wiki/Cronyism" title="Cronyism">cronies</a> of the military junta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IncomeGap_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-IncomeGap-23">[21]</a></sup> As of 2020<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Myanmar&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, according to the <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">Human Development Index</a>, Myanmar ranks 147 out of 189 countries in terms of human development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HDI_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-HDI-9">[9]</a></sup> Since 2021, more than 600,000 people were displaced across Myanmar due to the surge in violence post-coup, with more than 3 million people in dire need of humanitarian assistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[22]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Names_of_Myanmar" title="Names of Myanmar">Names of Myanmar</a></div>
<p>The name of the country has been a matter of dispute and disagreement, particularly in the early 21st century, focusing mainly on the political legitimacy of those using <i>Myanmar</i> versus <i>Burma</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[24]</a></sup> Both names derive from the earlier <a href="/wiki/Burmese_language" title="Burmese language">Burmese</a> <i>Mranma</i> or <i>Mramma</i>, an ethnonym for the majority <a href="/wiki/Bamar_people" title="Bamar people">Burman</a> ethnic group, of uncertain etymology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dgeh_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-dgeh-27">[25]</a></sup> The terms are also popularly thought to derive from <i>Brahma Desha</i> or  à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¹à¥à¤®à¤¾à¤¦à¥à¤¶/à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¹à¥à¤®à¤¾à¤µà¤°à¥à¤¤ (<a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>) after <a href="/wiki/Brahma" title="Brahma">Brahma</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1989, the military government <a href="/wiki/List_of_renamed_places_in_Myanmar" title="List of renamed places in Myanmar">officially changed</a> the English translations of many names dating back to <a href="/wiki/British_rule_in_Burma" title="British rule in Burma">Burma's colonial period</a> or earlier, including that of the country itself: <i>Burma</i> became <i>Myanmar</i>. The renaming remains a contested issue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Houtman1999_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Houtman1999-29">[27]</a></sup> Many political and ethnic opposition groups and countries continue to use <i>Burma</i> because they do not recognise the legitimacy of the ruling military government or its authority to rename the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-steinberg_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-steinberg-30">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>In April 2016, soon after taking office, <a href="/wiki/Aung_San_Suu_Kyi" title="Aung San Suu Kyi">Aung San Suu Kyi</a> commented on the question of which name should be used and said that "it is up to you because there is nothing in the constitution of our country that says that you must use any term in particular". She continued, "I use Burma very often because I am used to using it. But it does not mean that I require other people to do that as well. And I'll make an effort to say Myanmar from time to time so you all feel comfortable."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scmp_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-scmp-31">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>The country's official full name is "Republic of the Union of Myanmar" (<a href="/wiki/Burmese_language" title="Burmese language">Burmese</a>: <span lang="my">áá¼ááºáá±á¬ááºáá¯ááá¹áá áá¼ááºáá¬áá­á¯ááºáá¶áá±á¬áº</span>, <i><span title="Burmese-language romanization"><i lang="my-Latn">Pyihtaungsu Thamada Myanma Naingngantaw</i></span></i>, <small>pronouncedÂ </small><span class="IPA" lang="my-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Burmese" title="Help:IPA/Burmese">[pjÃ¬dÃ ÊÉ´zá¹µ Î¸Ã É´mÉdaÌ° mjÉmÃ  nÃ ÉªÉ´ÅÃ É´dÉÌ]</a></span>). Countries that do not officially recognise that name use the long form "Union of Burma" instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World_Factbook_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-World_Factbook-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCGUB_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-NCGUB-32">[30]</a></sup> In English, the country is popularly known as either <i>Burma</i> or <i>Myanmar</i>. In Burmese, the pronunciation depends on the <a href="/wiki/Register_(sociolinguistics)" title="Register (sociolinguistics)">register</a> used and is either <i><span title="Burmese-language romanization"><i lang="my-Latn">Bama</i></span></i> (<small>pronouncedÂ </small><span class="IPA" lang="my-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Burmese" title="Help:IPA/Burmese">[bÉmÃ ]</a></span>) or <i><span title="Burmese-language romanization"><i lang="my-Latn">Myamah</i></span></i> (<small>pronouncedÂ </small><span class="IPA" lang="my-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Burmese" title="Help:IPA/Burmese">[mjÉmÃ ]</a></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Houtman1999_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Houtman1999-29">[27]</a></sup> The name <i>Burma</i> has been in use in English since the 18th century.
</p><p>Official United States policy retains <i>Burma</i> as the country's name although the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_State" title="United States Department of State">State Department</a>'s website lists the country as <i>Burma (Myanmar)</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USNaming_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-USNaming-33">[31]</a></sup> The CIA's <i><a href="/wiki/The_World_Factbook" title="The World Factbook">World Factbook</a></i> lists the country as <i>Burma</i> as of FebruaryÂ 2021<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Myanmar&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World_Factbook_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-World_Factbook-2">[2]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Canada" title="Government of Canada">government of Canada</a> has in the past used <i>Burma</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dittmer_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dittmer-34">[32]</a></sup> such as in its 2007 legislation imposing sanctions<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[33]</a></sup> but as of August 2020 generally uses <i>Myanmar</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[34]</a></sup> The Czech Republic officially uses <i>Myanmar</i>, although its <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Foreign_Affairs_(Czech_Republic)" title="Ministry of Foreign Affairs (Czech Republic)">Ministry of Foreign Affairs</a> uses both <i>Myanmar</i> and <i>Burma</i> on its website.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[35]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> uses <i>Myanmar</i>, as does the ASEAN and as do Australia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[36]</a></sup> Russia, Germany,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[37]</a></sup> China, India, Bangladesh, Norway,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[38]</a></sup> Japan<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dittmer_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dittmer-34">[32]</a></sup> and Switzerland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[39]</a></sup> Most English-speaking international news media refer to the country by the name <i>Myanmar</i>, including the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[40]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/CNN" title="CNN">CNN</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[41]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Al_Jazeera" title="Al Jazeera">Al Jazeera</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[42]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Reuters" title="Reuters">Reuters</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[43]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Broadcasting_Corporation" title="Australian Broadcasting Corporation">Australian Broadcasting Corporation</a> (ABC)/<a href="/wiki/Radio_Australia" title="Radio Australia">Radio Australia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ABCRA_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-ABCRA-46">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Myanmar is known by a name deriving from <i>Burma</i> as opposed to <i>Myanmar</i> in <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romanian_language" title="Romanian language">Romanian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> â <i>Birmania</i> being the local version of <i>Burma</i> in both Italian and Spanish, <i>BirmÃ¢nia</i> in Portuguese, and <i>Birmanie</i> in French.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[45]</a></sup> As in the past, French-language media today consistently use <i>Birmanie</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is no established pronunciation of the English name <i>Myanmar</i>, and at least nine different pronunciations exist. Those with two syllables are listed as more common by major UK and US dictionaries except Collins: <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/Ë/: secondary stress follows">Ë</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/j/: 'y' in 'yes'">j</span><span title="/Ã¦/: 'a' in 'bad'">Ã¦</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/Ë/: primary stress follows">Ë</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ÉËr/: 'ar' in 'far'">ÉËr</span></span>/</a></span></span>, <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/Ë/: primary stress follows">Ë</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/j/: 'y' in 'yes'">j</span><span title="/Ã¦/: 'a' in 'bad'">Ã¦</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ÉËr/: 'ar' in 'far'">ÉËr</span></span>/</a></span></span>, <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/Ë/: secondary stress follows">Ë</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/j/: 'y' in 'yes'">j</span><span title="/ÉË/: 'a' in 'father'">ÉË</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/Ë/: primary stress follows">Ë</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ÉËr/: 'ar' in 'far'">ÉËr</span></span>/</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:En-us-Myanmar.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="" 2x" width="11"/></a></span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/44/En-us-Myanmar.ogg" title="En-us-Myanmar.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span></span>, <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/Ë/: primary stress follows">Ë</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/j/: 'y' in 'yes'">j</span><span title="/ÉË/: 'a' in 'father'">ÉË</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ÉËr/: 'ar' in 'far'">ÉËr</span></span>/</a></span></span>. Dictionaries and other sources also report pronunciations with three syllables <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/Ë/: primary stress follows">Ë</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/iË/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iË</span><span title="/É/: 'a' in 'about'">É</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ÉËr/: 'ar' in 'far'">ÉËr</span></span>/</a></span></span>, <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span><span title="/Ë/: primary stress follows">Ë</span><span title="/Ã¦/: 'a' in 'bad'">Ã¦</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ÉËr/: 'ar' in 'far'">ÉËr</span></span>/</a></span></span>, <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/Ë/: secondary stress follows">Ë</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/aÉª/: 'i' in 'tide'">aÉª</span><span title="/É/: 'a' in 'about'">É</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/Ë/: primary stress follows">Ë</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ÉËr/: 'ar' in 'far'">ÉËr</span></span>/</a></span></span>, <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/aÉª/: 'i' in 'tide'">aÉª</span><span title="/Ë/: primary stress follows">Ë</span><span title="/ÉË/: 'a' in 'father'">ÉË</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ÉËr/: 'ar' in 'far'">ÉËr</span></span>/</a></span></span>, <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/Ë/: primary stress follows">Ë</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/aÉª/: 'i' in 'tide'">aÉª</span><span title="/Ã¦/: 'a' in 'bad'">Ã¦</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ÉËr/: 'ar' in 'far'">ÉËr</span></span>/</a></span></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Myanmar_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Myanmar-50">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>As <a href="/wiki/John_C._Wells" title="John C. Wells">John Wells</a> explains, the English spellings of both Myanmar and Burma assume a non-rhotic variety of English, in which the letter r before a consonant or finally serves merely to indicate a long vowel: [ËmjÃ¦nmÉË, ËbÉËmÉ]. So the pronunciation of the last syllable of Myanmar as [mÉËr] or of Burma as [bÉËrmÉ] by some speakers in the UK and most speakers in North America is in fact a <a href="/wiki/Spelling_pronunciation" title="Spelling pronunciation">spelling pronunciation</a> based on a misunderstanding of non-rhotic spelling conventions. The final <i>r</i> in <i>Myanmar</i> was not intended for pronunciation and is there to ensure that the final a is pronounced with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Broad_a" title="Broad a">broad <i>ah</i></a> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ÉË/: 'a' in 'father'">ÉË</span></span>/</a></span></span>) in "father". If the Burmese name <span title="Burmese-language text"><span lang="my">áá¼ááºáá¬</span></span> <small></small><span class="IPA" lang="my-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Burmese" title="Help:IPA/Burmese">[mjÉmÃ ]</a></span> were spelled "Myanma" in English, this would be pronounced <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/É/: 'a' in 'about'">É</span></span>/</a></span></span> at the end by all English speakers. If it were spelled "Myanmah", the end would be pronounced <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ÉË/: 'a' in 'father'">ÉË</span></span>/</a></span></span> by all English speakers.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>

<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Myanmar" title="History of Myanmar">History of Myanmar</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory">Prehistory</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Prehistory_of_Myanmar" title="Prehistory of Myanmar">Prehistory of Myanmar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Migration_period_of_ancient_Burma" title="Migration period of ancient Burma">Migration period of ancient Burma</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pyu_Realm.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="583" data-file-width="264" decoding="async" height="486" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Pyu_Realm.png/220px-Pyu_Realm.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/49/Pyu_Realm.png 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pyu_Realm.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Pyu_city-states" title="Pyu city-states">Pyu city-states</a>, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">â8th century</span>; <a href="/wiki/Bagan" title="Bagan">Pagan</a> is shown for comparison only and is not contemporary.</div></div></div>
<p>Archaeological evidence shows that <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a></i> lived in the region now known as Myanmar as early as 750,000 years ago, with no more <i>erectus</i> finds after 75,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BLibConfOBMS2015_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-BLibConfOBMS2015-51">[49]</a></sup> The first evidence of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i> is dated to about 25,000 BP with discoveries of stone tools in central Myanmar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[50]</a></sup> Evidence of <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> age domestication of plants and animals and the use of polished stone tools dating to sometime between 10,000 and 6,000 BCE has been discovered in the form of <a href="/wiki/Cave_painting" title="Cave painting">cave paintings</a> in <a href="/wiki/Padah-Lin_Caves" title="Padah-Lin Caves">Padah-Lin Caves</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> arrived <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">â1500 BCE</span> when people in the region were turning copper into bronze, growing rice and domesticating poultry and pigs; they were among the first people in the world to do so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[52]</a></sup> Human remains and artefacts from this era were discovered in <a href="/wiki/Monywa_District" title="Monywa District">Monywa District</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Sagaing_Region" title="Sagaing Region">Sagaing Region</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[53]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> began around 500 BCE with the emergence of iron-working settlements in an area south of present-day Mandalay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[54]</a></sup> Evidence also shows the presence of rice-growing settlements of large villages and small towns that traded with their surroundings as far as China between 500 BCE and 200 CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[55]</a></sup> Iron Age Burmese cultures also had influences from outside sources such as India and <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>, as seen in their funerary practices concerning child burials. This indicates some form of communication between groups in Myanmar and other places, possibly through trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_city-states">Early city-states</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Pyu_city-states" title="Pyu city-states">Pyu city-states</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mon_kingdoms" title="Mon kingdoms">Mon kingdoms</a></div>
<p>Around the second century BCE the first-known <a href="/wiki/City-state" title="City-state">city-states</a> emerged in central Myanmar. The city-states were founded as part of the southward migration by the Tibeto-Burman-speaking Pyu people, the earliest inhabitants of Myanmar of whom records are extant, from present-day <a href="/wiki/Yunnan" title="Yunnan">Yunnan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EarlyYunnan_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-EarlyYunnan-59">[57]</a></sup> The Pyu culture was heavily influenced by trade with India, importing Buddhism as well as other cultural, architectural and political concepts, which would have an enduring influence on later Burmese culture and political organisation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the 9th century, several city-states had sprouted across the land: the Pyu in the central dry zone, Mon along the southern coastline and Arakanese along the western littoral. The balance was upset when the Pyu came under repeated attacks from <a href="/wiki/Nanzhao" title="Nanzhao">Nanzhao</a> between the 750s and the 830s. In the mid-to-late 9th century the <a href="/wiki/Bamar_people" title="Bamar people">Bamar people</a> founded a small settlement at <a href="/wiki/Bagan" title="Bagan">Bagan</a>. It was one of several competing city-states until the late 10th century, when it grew in authority and grandeur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pagan_Kingdom">Pagan Kingdom</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Pagan_Kingdom" title="Pagan Kingdom">Pagan Kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toungoo_dynasty" title="Toungoo dynasty">Toungoo dynasty</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Konbaung_dynasty" title="Konbaung dynasty">Konbaung dynasty</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ava" title="Kingdom of Ava">Kingdom of Ava</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hanthawaddy_Kingdom" title="Hanthawaddy Kingdom">Hanthawaddy Kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Mrauk_U" title="Kingdom of Mrauk U">Kingdom of Mrauk U</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Shan_States" title="Shan States">Shan States</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bagan,_Burma.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="931" data-file-width="1424" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Bagan%2C_Burma.jpg/220px-Bagan%2C_Burma.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Bagan%2C_Burma.jpg/330px-Bagan%2C_Burma.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Bagan%2C_Burma.jpg/440px-Bagan%2C_Burma.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bagan,_Burma.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Pagoda" title="Pagoda">Pagodas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kyaung" title="Kyaung">kyaungs</a> in present-day <a href="/wiki/Bagan" title="Bagan">Bagan</a>, the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Pagan_Kingdom" title="Pagan Kingdom">Pagan Kingdom</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pagan_Kingdom" title="Pagan Kingdom">Pagan</a> gradually grew to absorb its surrounding states until the 1050sâ1060s when <a href="/wiki/Anawrahta" title="Anawrahta">Anawrahta</a> founded the <a href="/wiki/Pagan_Kingdom" title="Pagan Kingdom">Pagan Kingdom</a>, the first ever unification of the Irrawaddy valley and its periphery. In the 12th and 13th centuries, the Pagan Empire and the <a href="/wiki/Khmer_Empire" title="Khmer Empire">Khmer Empire</a> were two main powers in mainland Southeast Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[60]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Burmese_language" title="Burmese language">Burmese language</a> and culture gradually became dominant in the upper Irrawaddy valley, eclipsing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyu_language_(Burma)" title="Pyu language (Burma)">Pyu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mon_language" title="Mon language">Mon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pali" title="Pali">Pali</a> norms by the late 12th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mha-63-65_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-mha-63-65-63">[61]</a></sup> Theravada <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> slowly began to spread to the village level, although <a href="/wiki/Vajrayana" title="Vajrayana">Tantric</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mahayana" title="Mahayana">Mahayana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Burmese_folk_religion" title="Burmese folk religion">folk religion</a> remained heavily entrenched. Pagan's rulers and wealthy built over 10,000 <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhist</a> temples in the Pagan capital zone alone. Repeated Mongol invasions in the late 13th century toppled the four-century-old kingdom in 1287.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mha-63-65_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-mha-63-65-63">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MysticalMraukU.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="533" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/MysticalMraukU.jpg/220px-MysticalMraukU.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/MysticalMraukU.jpg/330px-MysticalMraukU.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/MysticalMraukU.jpg/440px-MysticalMraukU.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MysticalMraukU.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Temples at <a href="/wiki/Mrauk_U" title="Mrauk U">Mrauk U</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Pagan's collapse was followed by 250 years of political fragmentation that lasted well into the 16th century. Like the Burmans four centuries earlier, <a href="/wiki/Shan_people" title="Shan people">Shan migrants</a> who arrived with the Mongol invasions stayed behind. Several competing <a href="/wiki/Shan_States" title="Shan States">Shan States</a> came to dominate the entire northwestern to eastern arc surrounding the Irrawaddy valley. The valley too was beset with petty states until the late 14th century when two sizeable powers, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ava" title="Kingdom of Ava">Ava Kingdom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hanthawaddy_Kingdom" title="Hanthawaddy Kingdom">Hanthawaddy Kingdom</a>, emerged. In the west, a politically fragmented Arakan was under competing influences of its stronger neighbours until the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Mrauk_U" title="Kingdom of Mrauk U">Kingdom of Mrauk U</a> unified the Arakan coastline for the first time in 1437. The kingdom was a protectorate of the <a href="/wiki/Bengal_Sultanate" title="Bengal Sultanate">Bengal Sultanate</a> at different time periods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kh-2-25_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-kh-2-25-64">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 14th and 15th centuries, Ava fought <a href="/wiki/Forty_Years%27_War" title="Forty Years' War">wars of unification</a> but could never quite reassemble the lost empire. Having held off Ava, the <a href="/wiki/Mon_people" title="Mon people">Mon</a>-speaking Hanthawaddy entered its golden age, and Arakan went on to become a power in its own right for the next 350 years. In contrast, constant warfare left Ava greatly weakened, and it slowly disintegrated from 1481 onward. In 1527, the Confederation of Shan States conquered Ava and ruled Upper Myanmar until 1555.
</p><p>Like the Pagan Empire, Ava, <a href="/wiki/Bago,_Myanmar" title="Bago, Myanmar">Hanthawaddy</a> and the Shan states were all <a href="/wiki/Multiracial_people" title="Multiracial people">multi-ethnic</a> polities. Despite the wars, cultural synchronisation continued. This period is considered a golden age for <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Myanmar" title="Culture of Myanmar">Burmese culture</a>. <a href="/wiki/Burmese_literature" title="Burmese literature">Burmese literature</a> "grew more confident, popular, and stylistically diverse", and the second generation of Burmese law codes as well as the earliest <a href="/wiki/Burmese_chronicles" title="Burmese chronicles">pan-Burma chronicles</a> emerged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[63]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bago,_Myanmar" title="Bago, Myanmar">Hanthawaddy</a> monarchs introduced religious reforms that later spread to the rest of the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[64]</a></sup> Many splendid temples of Mrauk U were built during this period.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taungoo_and_Konbaung">Taungoo and Konbaung</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Taungoo_Empire_(1580).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1121" data-file-width="950" decoding="async" height="260" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Map_of_Taungoo_Empire_%281580%29.png/220px-Map_of_Taungoo_Empire_%281580%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Map_of_Taungoo_Empire_%281580%29.png/330px-Map_of_Taungoo_Empire_%281580%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Map_of_Taungoo_Empire_%281580%29.png/440px-Map_of_Taungoo_Empire_%281580%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Taungoo_Empire_(1580).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/First_Toungoo_Empire" title="First Toungoo Empire">Toungoo Empire</a> under <a href="/wiki/Bayinnaung" title="Bayinnaung">Bayinnaung</a> in 1580</div></div></div>
<p>Political unification returned in the mid-16th century, through the efforts of <a href="/wiki/Toungoo_dynasty" title="Toungoo dynasty">Taungoo</a>, a former vassal state of Ava. Taungoo's young, ambitious King <a href="/wiki/Tabinshwehti" title="Tabinshwehti">Tabinshwehti</a> defeated the more powerful Hanthawaddy in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toungoo%E2%80%93Hanthawaddy_War_(1534%E2%80%931541)" title="ToungooâHanthawaddy War (1534â1541)">ToungooâHanthawaddy War</a>. His successor <a href="/wiki/Bayinnaung" title="Bayinnaung">Bayinnaung</a> went on to conquer a vast swath of mainland Southeast Asia including the Shan states, <a href="/wiki/Lan_Na" title="Lan Na">Lan Na</a>, Manipur, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mong_Mao" title="Mong Mao">Mong Mao</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ayutthaya_Kingdom" title="Ayutthaya Kingdom">Ayutthaya Kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lan_Xang" title="Lan Xang">Lan Xang</a> and southern Arakan. However, the largest empire in the history of Southeast Asia unravelled soon after Bayinnaung's death in 1581, completely collapsing by 1599. Ayutthaya seized Tenasserim and Lan Na, and Portuguese mercenaries established <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese rule</a> at <a href="/wiki/Thanlyin" title="Thanlyin">Thanlyin</a> (Syriam).
</p><p>
The dynasty regrouped and defeated the Portuguese in 1613 and Siam in 1614. It restored a smaller, more manageable kingdom, encompassing <a href="/wiki/Lower_Myanmar" title="Lower Myanmar">Lower Myanmar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Upper_Myanmar" title="Upper Myanmar">Upper Myanmar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shan_States" title="Shan States">Shan states</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lan_Na" title="Lan Na">Lan Na</a> and upper <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tenasserim_Division" title="Tenasserim Division">Tenasserim</a>. The restored Toungoo kings created a legal and political framework whose basic features continued well into the 19th century. The crown completely replaced the hereditary chieftainships with appointed governorships in the entire Irrawaddy valley and greatly reduced the hereditary rights of Shan chiefs. Its trade and secular administrative reforms built a prosperous economy for more than 80 years. From the 1720s onward, the kingdom was beset with repeated <a href="/wiki/Meitei_people" title="Meitei people">Meithei</a> raids into Upper Myanmar and a nagging rebellion in Lan Na. In 1740, the Mon of Lower Myanmar founded the <a href="/wiki/Restored_Hanthawaddy_Kingdom" title="Restored Hanthawaddy Kingdom">Restored Hanthawaddy Kingdom</a>. Hanthawaddy forces sacked Ava in 1752, ending the 266-year-old Toungoo Dynasty.</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shwedagon_pagoda.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5224" data-file-width="6276" decoding="async" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Shwedagon_pagoda.jpg/220px-Shwedagon_pagoda.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Shwedagon_pagoda.jpg/330px-Shwedagon_pagoda.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Shwedagon_pagoda.jpg/440px-Shwedagon_pagoda.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shwedagon_pagoda.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A British 1825 lithograph of <a href="/wiki/Shwedagon_Pagoda" title="Shwedagon Pagoda">Shwedagon Pagoda</a> shows British occupation during the <a href="/wiki/First_Anglo-Burmese_War" title="First Anglo-Burmese War">First Anglo-Burmese War</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>After the fall of Ava, the <a href="/wiki/Konbaung%E2%80%93Hanthawaddy_War" title="KonbaungâHanthawaddy War">KonbaungâHanthawaddy War</a> involved one resistance group under <a href="/wiki/Alaungpaya" title="Alaungpaya">Alaungpaya</a> defeating the Restored Hanthawaddy, and by 1759 he had reunited all of Myanmar and Manipur and driven out the <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">French</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">British</a>, who had provided arms to Hanthawaddy. By 1770, Alaungpaya's heirs had subdued much of Laos and fought and won the <a href="/wiki/Burmese%E2%80%93Siamese_War_(1765%E2%80%931767)" title="BurmeseâSiamese War (1765â1767)">BurmeseâSiamese War</a> against <a href="/wiki/Ayutthaya_Kingdom" title="Ayutthaya Kingdom">Ayutthaya</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sino-Burmese_War_(1765%E2%80%931769)" title="Sino-Burmese War (1765â1769)">Sino-Burmese War</a> against <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing China</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>With Burma preoccupied by the Chinese threat, Ayutthaya recovered its territories by 1770 and went on to capture Lan Na by 1776. Burma and Siam went to war until 1855, but all resulted in a stalemate, exchanging <a href="/wiki/Tenasserim_Island" title="Tenasserim Island">Tenasserim</a> (to Burma) and Lan Na (to Ayutthaya). Faced with a powerful China and a resurgent Ayutthaya in the east, King <a href="/wiki/Bodawpaya" title="Bodawpaya">Bodawpaya</a> turned west, acquiring Arakan (1785), Manipur (1814) and Assam (1817). It was the second-largest empire in Burmese history but also one with a long ill-defined border with <a href="/wiki/Presidencies_and_provinces_of_British_India" title="Presidencies and provinces of British India">British India</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>The breadth of this empire was short-lived. In 1826, <a href="/wiki/Burma_campaign" title="Burma campaign">Burma</a> lost Arakan, <a href="/wiki/Manipur" title="Manipur">Manipur</a>, Assam and Tenasserim to the British in the <a href="/wiki/First_Anglo-Burmese_War" title="First Anglo-Burmese War">First Anglo-Burmese War</a>. In 1852, the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British</a> easily seized Lower Burma in the <a href="/wiki/Second_Anglo-Burmese_War" title="Second Anglo-Burmese War">Second Anglo-Burmese War</a>. King <a href="/wiki/Mindon_Min" title="Mindon Min">Mindon Min</a> tried to modernise the kingdom and in 1875 narrowly avoided annexation by ceding the <a href="/wiki/Karenni_States" title="Karenni States">Karenni States</a>. The British, alarmed by the consolidation of <a href="/wiki/French_Indochina" title="French Indochina">French Indochina</a>, annexed the remainder of the country in the <a href="/wiki/Third_Anglo-Burmese_War" title="Third Anglo-Burmese War">Third Anglo-Burmese War</a> in 1885.
</p><p>Konbaung kings extended Restored Toungoo's administrative reforms and achieved unprecedented levels of internal control and external expansion. For the first time in history, the Burmese language and culture came to predominate the entire Irrawaddy valley. The evolution and growth of Burmese literature and theatre continued, aided by an extremely high adult male literacy rate for the era (half of all males and 5% of females).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[67]</a></sup> Nonetheless, the extent and pace of reforms were uneven and ultimately proved insufficient to stem the advance of British colonialism.
</p>
<h3><span id="British_Burma_.281885.E2.80.931948.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="British_Burma_(1885â1948)">British Burma (1885â1948)</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/British_rule_in_Burma" title="British rule in Burma">British rule in Burma</a> and <a href="/wiki/Burma_campaign" title="Burma campaign">Burma campaign</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:British_forces_arrival_mandalay1885.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="712" data-file-width="975" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/British_forces_arrival_mandalay1885.jpg/220px-British_forces_arrival_mandalay1885.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/British_forces_arrival_mandalay1885.jpg/330px-British_forces_arrival_mandalay1885.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/British_forces_arrival_mandalay1885.jpg/440px-British_forces_arrival_mandalay1885.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:British_forces_arrival_mandalay1885.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The landing of British forces in Mandalay after the last of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglo-Burmese_War" title="Anglo-Burmese War">Anglo-Burmese Wars</a>, which resulted in the abdication of the last Burmese monarch, King <a href="/wiki/Thibaw_Min" title="Thibaw Min">Thibaw Min</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:British_3-inch_mortar_detachments_support_the_19th_Indian_Division%27s_advance_along_the_Mawchi_Road,_east_of_Toungoo,_Burma.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="578" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/British_3-inch_mortar_detachments_support_the_19th_Indian_Division%27s_advance_along_the_Mawchi_Road%2C_east_of_Toungoo%2C_Burma.jpg/220px-British_3-inch_mortar_detachments_support_the_19th_Indian_Division%27s_advance_along_the_Mawchi_Road%2C_east_of_Toungoo%2C_Burma.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/British_3-inch_mortar_detachments_support_the_19th_Indian_Division%27s_advance_along_the_Mawchi_Road%2C_east_of_Toungoo%2C_Burma.jpg/330px-British_3-inch_mortar_detachments_support_the_19th_Indian_Division%27s_advance_along_the_Mawchi_Road%2C_east_of_Toungoo%2C_Burma.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/British_3-inch_mortar_detachments_support_the_19th_Indian_Division%27s_advance_along_the_Mawchi_Road%2C_east_of_Toungoo%2C_Burma.jpg/440px-British_3-inch_mortar_detachments_support_the_19th_Indian_Division%27s_advance_along_the_Mawchi_Road%2C_east_of_Toungoo%2C_Burma.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:British_3-inch_mortar_detachments_support_the_19th_Indian_Division%27s_advance_along_the_Mawchi_Road,_east_of_Toungoo,_Burma.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>British troops firing a <a href="/wiki/Mortar_(weapon)" title="Mortar (weapon)">mortar</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Mawchi" title="Mawchi">Mawchi</a> road, July 1944</div></div></div>
<p>In the 19th century, Burmese rulers, whose country had not previously been of particular interest to European traders, sought to maintain their traditional influence in the western areas of Assam, Manipur and Arakan. Pressing them, however, was the <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">British East India</a> Company, which was expanding its interests eastwards over the same territory. Over the next sixty years, diplomacy, raids, treaties and compromises, known collectively as the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Burmese_Wars" title="Anglo-Burmese Wars">Anglo-Burmese Wars</a>, continued until <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">Britain</a> proclaimed control over most of Burma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[68]</a></sup> With the fall of Mandalay, all of Burma came under British rule, being <a href="/wiki/Third_Anglo-Burmese_War#Annexation_and_resistance" title="Third Anglo-Burmese War">annexed</a> on 1 January 1886.
</p><p>Throughout the colonial era, many Indians arrived as soldiers, civil servants, construction workers and traders and, along with the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Burmese_people" title="Anglo-Burmese people">Anglo-Burmese</a> community, dominated commercial and civil life in Burma. <a href="/wiki/Yangon" title="Yangon">Rangoon</a> became the capital of British Burma and an important port between <a href="/wiki/Kolkata" title="Kolkata">Calcutta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>. Burmese resentment was strong, and was vented in violent riots that paralysed Rangoon on occasion until the 1930s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[69]</a></sup> Some of the discontent was caused by a disrespect for Burmese culture and traditions such as the British refusal to remove shoes when they entered pagodas. <a href="/wiki/Bhikkhu" title="Bhikkhu">Buddhist monks</a> became the vanguards of the independence movement. <a href="/wiki/U_Wisara" title="U Wisara">U Wisara</a>, an activist monk, died in prison after a 166-day hunger strike to protest against a rule that forbade him to wear his Buddhist robes while imprisoned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 1 April 1937, Burma became a separately administered colony of Great Britain, and <a href="/wiki/Ba_Maw" title="Ba Maw">Ba Maw</a> became the first Prime Minister and Premier of Burma. Ba Maw was an outspoken advocate for Burmese self-rule, and he opposed the participation of Great Britain, and by extension Burma, in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>. He resigned from the Legislative Assembly and was arrested for sedition. In 1940, before <a href="/wiki/Japan_during_World_War_II" title="Japan during World War II">Japan formally entered the war</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aung_San" title="Aung San">Aung San</a> formed the <a href="/wiki/Burma_Independence_Army" title="Burma Independence Army">Burma Independence Army</a> in Japan.
</p><p>As a major battleground, Burma was devastated during World War II by the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_invasion_of_Burma" title="Japanese invasion of Burma">Japanese invasion</a>. Within months after they entered the war, Japanese troops had advanced on Rangoon, and the British administration had collapsed. A <a href="/wiki/Japanese_occupation_of_Burma" title="Japanese occupation of Burma">Burmese Executive Administration</a> headed by Ba Maw was established by the Japanese in August 1942. <a href="/wiki/Orde_Wingate" title="Orde Wingate">Wingate</a>'s British <a href="/wiki/Chindits" title="Chindits">Chindits</a> were formed into <a href="/wiki/Long-range_penetration" title="Long-range penetration">long-range penetration</a> groups trained to operate deep behind Japanese lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[71]</a></sup> A similar <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">American</a> unit, <a href="/wiki/Merrill%27s_Marauders" title="Merrill's Marauders">Merrill's Marauders</a>, followed the Chindits into the Burmese jungle in 1943.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>Beginning in late 1944, allied troops launched a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burma_campaign_1944" title="Burma campaign 1944">series of offensives</a> that led to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burma_campaign_1944%E2%80%9345" title="Burma campaign 1944â45">end of Japanese rule</a> in July 1945. The battles were intense with much of Burma laid waste by the fighting. Overall, the Japanese lost some 150,000 men in Burma with 1,700 prisoners taken.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[73]</a></sup> Although many Burmese fought initially for the Japanese as part of the Burma Independence Army, many Burmese, mostly from the ethnic minorities, served in the British Burma Army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[74]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Burma_Independence_Army" title="Burma Independence Army">Burma National Army</a> and the Arakan National Army fought with the Japanese from 1942 to 1944 but switched allegiance to the Allied side in 1945. Overall, 170,000 to 250,000 Burmese civilians died during World War II.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aung_San" title="Aung San">Aung San</a> negotiated the <a href="/wiki/Panglong_Agreement" title="Panglong Agreement">Panglong Agreement</a> with ethnic leaders that guaranteed the independence of Myanmar as a unified state. <a href="/wiki/Aung_Zan_Wai" title="Aung Zan Wai">Aung Zan Wai</a>, Pe Khin, <a href="/wiki/Bo_Hmu_Aung" title="Bo Hmu Aung">Bo Hmu Aung</a>, Sir Maung Gyi, Dr. Sein Mya Maung, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myoma_U_Than_Kywe" title="Myoma U Than Kywe">Myoma U Than Kywe</a> were among the negotiators of the historic <a href="/wiki/Panglong_Conference" title="Panglong Conference">Panglong Conference</a> negotiated with Bamar leader General <a href="/wiki/Aung_San" title="Aung San">Aung San</a> and other ethnic leaders in 1947. In 1947, Aung San became Deputy Chairman of the Executive Council of Myanmar, a transitional government. But in July 1947, political rivals<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[76]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Burmese_Martyrs%27_Day" title="Burmese Martyrs' Day">assassinated Aung San</a> and several cabinet members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Independence_.281948.E2.80.931962.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Independence_(1948â1962)">Independence (1948â1962)</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Post-independence_Burma_(1948%E2%80%931962)" title="Post-independence Burma (1948â1962)">Post-independence Burma (1948â1962)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sao_Shwe_Thaik_and_Hubert_Elvin_Rance.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="435" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Sao_Shwe_Thaik_and_Hubert_Elvin_Rance.jpg/220px-Sao_Shwe_Thaik_and_Hubert_Elvin_Rance.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Sao_Shwe_Thaik_and_Hubert_Elvin_Rance.jpg/330px-Sao_Shwe_Thaik_and_Hubert_Elvin_Rance.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Sao_Shwe_Thaik_and_Hubert_Elvin_Rance.jpg/440px-Sao_Shwe_Thaik_and_Hubert_Elvin_Rance.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sao_Shwe_Thaik_and_Hubert_Elvin_Rance.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>British governor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hubert_Elvin_Rance" title="Hubert Elvin Rance">Hubert Elvin Rance</a> and Sao Shwe Thaik at the flag-raising ceremony on 4 January 1948 (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Independence_Day_of_Burma" title="Independence Day of Burma">Independence Day of Burma</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>On <a href="/wiki/Independence_Day_(Myanmar)" title="Independence Day (Myanmar)">4 January 1948</a>, the nation became an independent republic, under the terms of the <a href="/wiki/Burma_Independence_Act_1947" title="Burma Independence Act 1947">Burma Independence Act 1947</a>. The new country was named the <i>Union of Burma</i>, with <a href="/wiki/Sao_Shwe_Thaik" title="Sao Shwe Thaik">Sao Shwe Thaik</a> as its first president and <a href="/wiki/U_Nu" title="U Nu">U Nu</a> as its first prime minister. Unlike most other former British colonies and overseas territories, Burma did not become a member of the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Bicameralism" title="Bicameralism">bicameral</a> parliament was formed, consisting of a <a href="/wiki/Chamber_of_Deputies_(Burma)" title="Chamber of Deputies (Burma)">Chamber of Deputies</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Chamber_of_Nationalities" title="Chamber of Nationalities">Chamber of Nationalities</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[78]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Multi-party_system" title="Multi-party system">multi-party</a> elections were held in <a href="/wiki/1951%E2%80%9352_Burmese_general_election" title="1951â52 Burmese general election">1951â1952</a>, <a href="/wiki/1956_Burmese_general_election" title="1956 Burmese general election">1956</a> and <a href="/wiki/1960_Burmese_general_election" title="1960 Burmese general election">1960</a>.
</p><p>The geographical area Burma encompasses today can be traced to the <a href="/wiki/Panglong_Agreement" title="Panglong Agreement">Panglong Agreement</a>, which combined Burma Proper, which consisted of Lower Burma and Upper Burma, and the Frontier Areas, which had been administered separately by the British.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1961, <a href="/wiki/U_Thant" title="U Thant">U Thant</a>, the Union of Burma's Permanent Representative to the United Nations and former secretary to the prime minister, was elected <a href="/wiki/Secretary-General_of_the_United_Nations" title="Secretary-General of the United Nations">Secretary-General of the United Nations</a>, a position he held for ten years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[80]</a></sup> Among the Burmese to work at the UN when he was secretary-general was <a href="/wiki/Aung_San_Suu_Kyi" title="Aung San Suu Kyi">Aung San Suu Kyi</a> (daughter of Aung San), who went on to become winner of the 1991 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a>.
</p><p>When the non-Burman ethnic groups pushed for autonomy or federalism, alongside having a weak civilian government at the centre, the military leadership staged a coup d'Ã©tat in 1962. Though incorporated in the 1947 Constitution, successive military governments construed the use of the term '<a href="/wiki/Federalism" title="Federalism">federalism</a>' as being anti-national, anti-unity and pro-disintegration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Military_rule_.281962.E2.80.932011.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_rule_(1962â2011)">Military rule (1962â2011)</span></h3>
<p>On 2 March 1962, the military led by General <a href="/wiki/Ne_Win" title="Ne Win">Ne Win</a> <a href="/wiki/1962_Burmese_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1962 Burmese coup d'Ã©tat">took control of Burma through a coup d'Ã©tat</a>, and the government had been under direct or indirect control by the military since then. Between 1962 and 1974, Myanmar was ruled by a <a href="/wiki/Union_Revolutionary_Council" title="Union Revolutionary Council">revolutionary council</a> headed by the general. Almost all aspects of society (business, media, production) were <a href="/wiki/Nationalization" title="Nationalization">nationalised</a> or brought under government control under the <a href="/wiki/Burmese_Way_to_Socialism" title="Burmese Way to Socialism">Burmese Way to Socialism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thantmyintu_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-thantmyintu-84">[82]</a></sup> which combined Soviet-style nationalisation and <a href="/wiki/Economic_planning" title="Economic planning">central planning</a>.
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Myanmar" title="Constitution of Myanmar">new constitution</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Republic_of_the_Union_of_Burma" title="Socialist Republic of the Union of Burma">Socialist Republic of the Union of Burma</a> was adopted in 1974. Until 1988, the country was ruled as a <a href="/wiki/One-party_state" title="One-party state">one-party system</a>, with the general and other military officers resigning and ruling through the <a href="/wiki/Burma_Socialist_Programme_Party" title="Burma Socialist Programme Party">Burma Socialist Programme Party</a> (BSPP).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-christinafink_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-christinafink-85">[83]</a></sup> During this period, Myanmar became one of the world's most impoverished countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ruin_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-ruin-86">[84]</a></sup> There were sporadic protests against military rule during the Ne Win years, and these were almost always violently suppressed. On 7 July 1962, the government broke up <a href="/wiki/1962_Rangoon_University_protests" title="1962 Rangoon University protests">demonstrations at Rangoon University</a>, killing 15 students.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thantmyintu_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-thantmyintu-84">[82]</a></sup> In 1974, the military violently suppressed <a href="/wiki/U_Thant_funeral_crisis" title="U Thant funeral crisis">anti-government protests</a> at the funeral of U Thant. Student protests in 1975, 1976, and 1977 were quickly suppressed by overwhelming force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-christinafink_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-christinafink-85">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:8888_Uprising.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="264" data-file-width="377" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/30/8888_Uprising.jpg/220px-8888_Uprising.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/30/8888_Uprising.jpg/330px-8888_Uprising.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/3/30/8888_Uprising.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:8888_Uprising.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Protesters gathering in central <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rangoon" title="Rangoon">Rangoon</a>, 1988.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1988, unrest over economic mismanagement and political oppression by the government led to widespread pro-democracy demonstrations throughout the country known as the <a href="/wiki/8888_Uprising" title="8888 Uprising">8888 Uprising</a>. Security forces killed thousands of demonstrators, and General <a href="/wiki/Saw_Maung" title="Saw Maung">Saw Maung</a> staged a coup d'Ã©tat and formed the <a href="/wiki/State_Peace_and_Development_Council" title="State Peace and Development Council">State Law and Order Restoration Council</a> (SLORC). In 1989, SLORC declared martial law after widespread protests. The military government finalised plans for People's Assembly elections on 31 May 1989.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[85]</a></sup> SLORC changed the country's official English name from the "Socialist Republic of the Union of Burma" to the "Union of Myanmar" on 18 June 1989 by enacting the adaptation of the expression law.
</p><p>In May 1990, the government held free multiparty elections for the first time in almost 30 years, and the <a href="/wiki/National_League_for_Democracy" title="National League for Democracy">National League for Democracy</a> (NLD), the party of Aung San Suu Kyi, won<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-burmese_vote_1990_05_29_nytimes_com_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-burmese_vote_1990_05_29_nytimes_com-88">[86]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/1990_Myanmar_general_election" title="1990 Myanmar general election">earning 392 out of a total 492 seats</a> (i.e., 80% of the seats). However, the military junta refused to cede power<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[87]</a></sup> and continued to rule the nation, first as SLORC and, from 1997, as the State Peace and Development Council (SPDC) until its dissolution in March 2011. General <a href="/wiki/Than_Shwe" title="Than Shwe">Than Shwe</a> took over the Chairmanship â effectively the position of Myanmar's top ruler â from General Saw Maung in 1992 and held it until 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 23 June 1997, Myanmar was admitted into the Association of Southeast Asian Nations. On 27 March 2006, the military junta, which had moved the national capital from Yangon to a site near <a href="/wiki/Pyinmana" title="Pyinmana">Pyinmana</a> in November 2005, officially named the new capital <a href="/wiki/Naypyidaw" title="Naypyidaw">Naypyidaw</a>, meaning "city of the kings".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2007_Myanmar_protests_7.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/2007_Myanmar_protests_7.jpg/220px-2007_Myanmar_protests_7.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/2007_Myanmar_protests_7.jpg/330px-2007_Myanmar_protests_7.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/2007_Myanmar_protests_7.jpg/440px-2007_Myanmar_protests_7.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2007_Myanmar_protests_7.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Protesters in <a href="/wiki/Yangon" title="Yangon">Yangon</a> during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2007_Saffron_Revolution" title="2007 Saffron Revolution">2007 Saffron Revolution</a> with a banner that reads <i>non-violence: national movement</i> in <a href="/wiki/Burmese_language" title="Burmese language">Burmese</a>. In the background is <a href="/wiki/Shwedagon_Pagoda" title="Shwedagon Pagoda">Shwedagon Pagoda</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cyclone_Nargis_-Myanmar-3May2008.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Cyclone_Nargis_-Myanmar-3May2008.jpg/220px-Cyclone_Nargis_-Myanmar-3May2008.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Cyclone_Nargis_-Myanmar-3May2008.jpg/330px-Cyclone_Nargis_-Myanmar-3May2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Cyclone_Nargis_-Myanmar-3May2008.jpg/440px-Cyclone_Nargis_-Myanmar-3May2008.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cyclone_Nargis_-Myanmar-3May2008.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cyclone_Nargis" title="Cyclone Nargis">Cyclone Nargis</a> in southern Myanmar, May 2008.</div></div></div>
<p>In August 2007, an increase in the price of fuel led to the <a href="/wiki/Saffron_Revolution" title="Saffron Revolution">Saffron Revolution</a> led by Buddhist monks that were dealt with harshly by the government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PetrolSaffronRevolution_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-PetrolSaffronRevolution-92">[90]</a></sup> The government cracked down on them on 26 September 2007, with reports of barricades at the <a href="/wiki/Shwedagon_Pagoda" title="Shwedagon Pagoda">Shwedagon Pagoda</a> and monks killed. There were also rumours of disagreement within the Burmese armed forces, but none was confirmed. The military crackdown against unarmed <a href="/wiki/Protest" title="Protest">protesters</a> was widely condemned as part of the <a href="/wiki/International_reactions_to_the_Saffron_Revolution" title="International reactions to the Saffron Revolution">international reactions to the Saffron Revolution</a> and led to an increase in economic sanctions against the <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Myanmar" title="Politics of Myanmar">Burmese Government</a>.
</p><p>In May 2008, <a href="/wiki/Cyclone_Nargis" title="Cyclone Nargis">Cyclone Nargis</a> caused extensive damage in the densely populated rice-farming delta of the <a href="/wiki/Ayeyarwady_Region" title="Ayeyarwady Region">Irrawaddy Division</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[91]</a></sup> It was the worst natural disaster in Burmese history with reports of an estimated 200,000 people dead or missing, damages totalled to 10Â billion US dollars, and as many as 1 million were left homeless.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[92]</a></sup> In the critical days following this disaster, Myanmar's <a href="/wiki/Isolationism" title="Isolationism">isolationist</a> government was accused of hindering United Nations recovery efforts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[93]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Humanitarian_aid" title="Humanitarian aid">Humanitarian aid</a> was requested, but concerns about foreign military or intelligence presence in the country delayed the entry of United States military planes delivering medicine, food, and other supplies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>In early August 2009, <a href="/wiki/2009_Kokang_incident" title="2009 Kokang incident">a conflict broke out</a> in Shan State in northern Myanmar. For several weeks, junta troops fought against ethnic minorities including the <a href="/wiki/Kokang_Chinese" title="Kokang Chinese">Han Chinese</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[95]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wa_people" title="Wa people">Wa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jingpo_people" title="Jingpo people">Kachin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BangkokPost_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-BangkokPost-98">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTrefugees_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTrefugees-99">[97]</a></sup> During 8â12 August, the first days of the conflict, as many as 10,000 Burmese civilians fled to Yunnan in neighbouring China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BangkokPost_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-BangkokPost-98">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTrefugees_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYTrefugees-99">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_wars">Civil wars</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Internal_conflict_in_Myanmar" title="Internal conflict in Myanmar">Internal conflict in Myanmar</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Civil_war" title="Civil war">Civil wars</a> have been a constant feature of Myanmar's socio-political landscape since the attainment of independence in 1948. These wars are predominantly struggles for ethnic and sub-national autonomy, with the areas surrounding the ethnically Bamar central districts of the country serving as the primary geographical setting of conflict. Foreign journalists and visitors require a special travel permit to visit the areas in which Myanmar's civil wars continue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>In October 2012, the ongoing conflicts in Myanmar included the <a href="/wiki/Kachin_conflict" title="Kachin conflict">Kachin conflict</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[100]</a></sup> between the Pro-Christian <a href="/wiki/Kachin_Independence_Army" title="Kachin Independence Army">Kachin Independence Army</a> and the government;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChristianVsGovt_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-ChristianVsGovt-103">[101]</a></sup> a civil war between the <a href="/wiki/Rohingya_people" title="Rohingya people">Rohingya</a> Muslims and the government and non-government groups in <a href="/wiki/Rakhine_State" title="Rakhine State">Rakhine State</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[102]</a></sup> and a conflict between the <a href="/wiki/Shan_people" title="Shan people">Shan</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ShanVsGovt_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-ShanVsGovt-105">[103]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lahu_people" title="Lahu people">Lahu</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Karen_people" title="Karen people">Karen</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[105]</a></sup> minority groups, and the government in the eastern half of the country. In addition, <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda" title="Al-Qaeda">al-Qaeda</a> signalled an intention to become involved in Myanmar. In a video released on 3 September 2014, mainly addressed to India, the militant group's leader <a href="/wiki/Ayman_al-Zawahiri" title="Ayman al-Zawahiri">Ayman al-Zawahiri</a> said al-Qaeda had not forgotten the Muslims of Myanmar and that the group was doing "what they can to rescue you".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[106]</a></sup> In response, the military raised its level of alertness, while the Burmese Muslim Association issued a statement saying Muslims would not tolerate any threat to their motherland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>Armed conflict between <a href="/wiki/Myanmar_National_Democratic_Alliance_Army" title="Myanmar National Democratic Alliance Army">ethnic Chinese rebels</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Tatmadaw" title="Tatmadaw">Myanmar Armed Forces</a> resulted in the <a href="/wiki/2015_Kokang_offensive" title="2015 Kokang offensive">Kokang offensive</a> in February 2015.
