<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ethnolinguistic_map_of_Burma_1972_en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2037" data-file-width="1063" decoding="async" height="422" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Ethnolinguistic_map_of_Burma_1972_en.svg/220px-Ethnolinguistic_map_of_Burma_1972_en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Ethnolinguistic_map_of_Burma_1972_en.svg/330px-Ethnolinguistic_map_of_Burma_1972_en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Ethnolinguistic_map_of_Burma_1972_en.svg/440px-Ethnolinguistic_map_of_Burma_1972_en.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ethnolinguistic_map_of_Burma_1972_en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ethnolinguistic Groups of Burma/Myanmar</div></div></div>
<p>Myanmar is <a href="/wiki/Multiculturalism" title="Multiculturalism">ethnically diverse</a>. The government recognises <a href="/wiki/List_of_ethnic_groups_in_Myanmar" title="List of ethnic groups in Myanmar">135 distinct ethnic groups</a>. There are at least 108 different ethnolinguistic groups in Myanmar, consisting mainly of distinct <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tibeto-Burman" title="Tibeto-Burman">Tibeto-Burman</a> peoples, but with sizeable populations of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tai%E2%80%93Kadai-speaking_peoples" title="TaiâKadai-speaking peoples">TaiâKadai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hmong%E2%80%93Mien_languages" title="HmongâMien languages">HmongâMien</a>, and Austroasiatic (MonâKhmer) peoples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-316"><a href="#cite_note-316">[314]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Bamar_people" title="Bamar people">Bamar</a> form an estimated 68% of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-statedept_317-0"><a href="#cite_note-statedept-317">[315]</a></sup> 10% of the population are <a href="/wiki/Shan_people" title="Shan people">Shan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-statedept_317-1"><a href="#cite_note-statedept-317">[315]</a></sup> The Kayin make up 7% of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-statedept_317-2"><a href="#cite_note-statedept-317">[315]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Rakhine_people" title="Rakhine people">Rakhine people</a> constitute 4% of the population. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burmese_Chinese" title="Burmese Chinese">Overseas Chinese</a> form approximately 3% of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-statedept_317-3"><a href="#cite_note-statedept-317">[315]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-318"><a href="#cite_note-318">[316]</a></sup> Myanmar's ethnic <a href="/wiki/Minority_group" title="Minority group">minority</a> groups prefer the term "ethnic nationality" over "ethnic minority" as the term "minority" furthers their sense of insecurity in the face of what is often described as "Burmanisation"âthe proliferation and domination of the dominant <a href="/wiki/Bamar_people" title="Bamar people">Bamar culture</a> over minority cultures.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Mon_people" title="Mon people">Mon</a>, who form 2% of the population, are ethno-linguistically related to the <a href="/wiki/Khmer_people" title="Khmer people">Khmer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-statedept_317-4"><a href="#cite_note-statedept-317">[315]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Burmese_Indians" title="Burmese Indians">Overseas Indians</a> are 2%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-statedept_317-5"><a href="#cite_note-statedept-317">[315]</a></sup> The remainder are <a href="/wiki/Kachin_people" title="Kachin people">Kachin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chin_people" title="Chin people">Chin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rohingya_people" title="Rohingya people">Rohingya</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglo-Indian" title="Anglo-Indian">Anglo-Indians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Burmese_Gurkha" title="Burmese Gurkha">Gurkha</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People_of_Nepal" title="People of Nepal">Nepali</a> and other ethnic minorities. Included in this group are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglo-Burmese" title="Anglo-Burmese">Anglo-Burmese</a>. Once forming a large and influential community, the Anglo-Burmese left the country in steady streams from 1958 onwards, principally to Australia and the United Kingdom. It is estimated that 52,000 Anglo-Burmese remain in Myanmar. As of 2009<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Myanmar&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, 110,000 Burmese <a href="/wiki/Refugee" title="Refugee">refugees</a> were living in refugee camps in Thailand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-319"><a href="#cite_note-319">[317]</a></sup>
</p><p>Refugee camps exist along Indian, Bangladeshi and Thai borders while several thousand are in <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>. Conservative estimates state that there are over 295,800 minority refugees from Myanmar, with the majority being <a href="/wiki/Rohingya_people" title="Rohingya people">Rohingya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karen_people" title="Karen people">Karen</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_Karen" title="Red Karen">Karenni</a> are principally located along the Thai-Myanmar border.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-320"><a href="#cite_note-320">[318]</a></sup> There are nine permanent refugee camps along the Thai-Myanmar border, most of which were established in the mid-1980s. The refugee camps are under the care of the Thai-Burma Border Consortium (TBBC). Since 2006,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-321"><a href="#cite_note-321">[319]</a></sup> over 55,000 Burmese <a href="/wiki/Refugee" title="Refugee">refugees</a> have been resettled in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-322"><a href="#cite_note-322">[320]</a></sup>
</p><p>The persecution of <a href="/wiki/Burmese_Indians" title="Burmese Indians">Burmese Indians</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burmese_Chinese" title="Burmese Chinese">Burmese Chinese</a> and other ethnic groups after the military coup headed by General <a href="/wiki/Ne_Win" title="Ne Win">Ne Win</a> in 1962 led to the expulsion or emigration of 300,000 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-323"><a href="#cite_note-323">[321]</a></sup> They migrated to escape <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Racial_Discrimination_against_Burmese_Indians" title="Racial Discrimination against Burmese Indians">racial discrimination</a> and the wholesale nationalisation of private enterprise that took place in 1964.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-324"><a href="#cite_note-324">[322]</a></sup> The Anglo-Burmese at this time either fled the country or changed their names and blended in with the broader Burmese society.
</p><p>Many <a href="/wiki/Rohingya_people" title="Rohingya people">Rohingya</a> Muslims have fled Myanmar. Many refugees headed to neighbouring Bangladesh, including 200,000 in 1978 as a result of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_Dragon_operation_in_Arakan" title="King Dragon operation in Arakan">King Dragon operation in Arakan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-325"><a href="#cite_note-325">[323]</a></sup> 250,000 more left in 1991.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-326"><a href="#cite_note-326">[324]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Myanmar" title="Languages of Myanmar">Languages of Myanmar</a></div>
<p>Myanmar is home to four major language families: <a href="/wiki/Sino-Tibetan_languages" title="Sino-Tibetan languages">Sino-Tibetan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tai%E2%80%93Kadai_languages" title="TaiâKadai languages">TaiâKadai</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Austro-Asiatic_languages" title="Austro-Asiatic languages">Austro-Asiatic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-327"><a href="#cite_note-327">[325]</a></sup> Sino-Tibetan languages are most widely spoken. They include <a href="/wiki/Burmese_language" title="Burmese language">Burmese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karen_people" title="Karen people">Karen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jingpo_people" title="Jingpo people">Kachin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chin_people" title="Chin people">Chin</a>, and Chinese (mainly <a href="/wiki/Hokkien" title="Hokkien">Hokkien</a>). The primary TaiâKadai language is <a href="/wiki/Shan_language" title="Shan language">Shan</a>. <a href="/wiki/Mon_language" title="Mon language">Mon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palaung_language" title="Palaung language">Palaung</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Va_people" title="Va people">Wa</a> are the major <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Austroasiatic" title="Austroasiatic">Austroasiatic</a> languages spoken in Myanmar. The two major Indo-European languages are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/P%C4%81li" title="PÄli">Pali</a>, the liturgical language of Theravada Buddhism, and English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ethno_328-0"><a href="#cite_note-ethno-328">[326]</a></sup> More than a hundred languages are spoken in total. Since many of them are known only within small tribes around the country, they may have been lost (many if not all) after a few generations.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Burmese_language" title="Burmese language">Burmese</a>, the mother tongue of the Bamar and official language of Myanmar, is related to <a href="/wiki/Tibetic_languages" title="Tibetic languages">Tibetan</a> and Chinese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ethno_328-1"><a href="#cite_note-ethno-328">[326]</a></sup> It is written in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burmese_script" title="Burmese script">script</a> consisting of circular and semi-circular letters, which were adapted from the <a href="/wiki/Mon_language" title="Mon language">Mon script</a>, which in turn was developed from a southern Indian script in the 5th century. The earliest known inscriptions in the Burmese script date from the 11th century. It is also used to write <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/P%C4%81li" title="PÄli">Pali</a>, the sacred language of Theravada Buddhism, as well as several ethnic minority languages, including Shan, several Karen dialects, and Kayah (Karenni), with the addition of specialised characters and <a href="/wiki/Diacritic" title="Diacritic">diacritics</a> for each language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-329"><a href="#cite_note-329">[327]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Burmese language incorporates widespread usage of <a href="/wiki/Honorific" title="Honorific">honorifics</a> and is age-oriented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Myam-ma_330-0"><a href="#cite_note-Myam-ma-330">[328]</a></sup> Burmese society has traditionally stressed the importance of education. In villages, secular schooling often takes place in <a href="/wiki/Monastery" title="Monastery">monasteries</a>. Secondary and tertiary education take place at government schools.
</p>

<p>Many religions are practised in Myanmar. Religious edifices and orders have been in existence for many years. The Christian and Muslim populations do, however, face religious persecution and it is hard, if not impossible, for non-Buddhists to join the army or get government jobs, the main route to success in the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-333"><a href="#cite_note-333">[330]</a></sup> Such persecution and targeting of civilians is particularly notable in eastern Myanmar, where over 3,000 villages have been destroyed in the past ten years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-334"><a href="#cite_note-334">[331]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-335"><a href="#cite_note-335">[332]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-336"><a href="#cite_note-336">[333]</a></sup> More than 200,000 Muslims have fled to Bangladesh by 2007 to escape persecution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-337"><a href="#cite_note-337">[334]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-338"><a href="#cite_note-338">[335]</a></sup>
</p><p>A large majority of the population practices Buddhism; estimates range from 80%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pew_339-0"><a href="#cite_note-pew-339">[336]</a></sup> to 89%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buddhanet_340-0"><a href="#cite_note-Buddhanet-340">[337]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/2014_Myanmar_Census" title="2014 Myanmar Census">2014 Myanmar Census</a>, 87.9% of the population identifies as Buddhists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TUR_331-1"><a href="#cite_note-TUR-331">[329]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Therav%C4%81da" title="TheravÄda">TheravÄda</a> Buddhism is the most widespread.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buddhanet_340-1"><a href="#cite_note-Buddhanet-340">[337]</a></sup> There are some 500,000 Buddhist monks and 75,000 nuns in this country of 54 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-341"><a href="#cite_note-341">[338]</a></sup> Other religions are practised largely without obstruction, with the notable exception of some religious minorities such as the Rohingya people, who have continued to have their citizenship status denied and treated as illegal immigrants instead,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rohingya_246-2"><a href="#cite_note-rohingya-246">[244]</a></sup> and Christians in Chin State.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-342"><a href="#cite_note-342">[339]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to 2014 census, 6.2% of the population identifies as Christian; 4.3% as Muslim; 0.8% as followers of tribal religions; 0.5% as <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hindus</a>; 0.2% as followers of other religions; and 0.1% follow no religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TUR_331-2"><a href="#cite_note-TUR-331">[329]</a></sup> According to the 2010 estimates of the <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a>, 7% of the population is Christian; 4% is Muslim; 1% follows traditional <a href="/wiki/Animism" title="Animism">animistic</a> beliefs; and 2% follow other religions, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mahayana_Buddhism" title="Mahayana Buddhism">Mahayana Buddhism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/East_Asian_religions" title="East Asian religions">East Asian religions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-343"><a href="#cite_note-343">[340]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-344"><a href="#cite_note-344">[341]</a></sup> Jehovah's Witnesses have been present since 1914<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-345"><a href="#cite_note-345">[342]</a></sup> and have about 80 congregations around the country and a branch office in Yangon publishing in 16 languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-346"><a href="#cite_note-346">[343]</a></sup> A tiny Jewish community in Yangon had a synagogue but no resident rabbi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-347"><a href="#cite_note-347">[344]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_nun_and_group_of_monks_praying_before_idols_in_Myanmar.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/A_nun_and_group_of_monks_praying_before_idols_in_Myanmar.jpg/220px-A_nun_and_group_of_monks_praying_before_idols_in_Myanmar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/A_nun_and_group_of_monks_praying_before_idols_in_Myanmar.jpg/330px-A_nun_and_group_of_monks_praying_before_idols_in_Myanmar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/A_nun_and_group_of_monks_praying_before_idols_in_Myanmar.jpg/440px-A_nun_and_group_of_monks_praying_before_idols_in_Myanmar.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_nun_and_group_of_monks_praying_before_idols_in_Myanmar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Praying Buddhist monks in <a href="/wiki/Shwedagon_Pagoda" title="Shwedagon Pagoda">Shwedagon Pagoda</a></div></div></div>
<p>Although Hinduism is practised by 0.5% of the population, it was a major religion in Myanmar's past. Several strains of Hinduism existed alongside both Theravada Buddhism and Mahayana Buddhism in the Mon and <a href="/wiki/Pyu_city-states" title="Pyu city-states">Pyu period</a> in the first millennium,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-348"><a href="#cite_note-348">[345]</a></sup> and down to the <a href="/wiki/Pagan_Kingdom" title="Pagan Kingdom">Pagan period</a> (9th to 13th centuries) when "<a href="/wiki/Shaivism" title="Shaivism">Saivite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vaishnavism" title="Vaishnavism">Vaishana</a> elements enjoyed greater elite influence than they would later do."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-349"><a href="#cite_note-349">[346]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Burmese_folk_religion" title="Burmese folk religion">Burmese folk religion</a> is practised by many <a href="/wiki/Bamar_people" title="Bamar people">Bamars</a> alongside Buddhism.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Myanmar" title="Health in Myanmar">Health in Myanmar</a></div>
<p>The general state of <a href="/wiki/Health_care" title="Health care">health care</a> in Myanmar is poor. The government spends anywhere from 0.5% to 3% of the country's GDP on health care, consistently ranking among the lowest in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-350"><a href="#cite_note-350">[347]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-351"><a href="#cite_note-351">[348]</a></sup> Although <a href="/wiki/Health_care" title="Health care">health care</a> is nominally free, in reality, patients have to pay for medicine and treatment, even in public clinics and hospitals. Public hospitals lack many of the basic facilities and equipment. The 2010 maternal mortality rate per 100,000 births for Myanmar is 240. This is compared with 219.3 in 2008 and 662 in 1990. The under 5 mortality rate, per 1,000 births is 73 and the neonatal mortality as a percentage of under 5's mortality is 47. According to the report named "Preventable Fate", published by Doctors without Borders, 25,000 Burmese AIDS patients died in 2007, deaths that could largely have been prevented by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antiretroviral_therapy" title="Antiretroviral therapy">antiretroviral therapy</a> drugs and proper treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_352-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-352">[349]</a></sup>
</p><p>HIV/AIDS, recognised as a disease of concern by the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Health_(Myanmar)" title="Ministry of Health (Myanmar)">Burmese Ministry of Health</a>, is most prevalent among <a href="/wiki/Sex_worker" title="Sex worker">sex workers</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intravenous_drug" title="Intravenous drug">intravenous drug</a> users. In 2005, the estimated adult <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS_in_Myanmar" title="HIV/AIDS in Myanmar">HIV prevalence rate in Myanmar</a> was 1.3% (200,000â570,000 people), according to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNAIDS" title="UNAIDS">UNAIDS</a>, and early indicators of any progress against the HIV epidemic are inconsistent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-353"><a href="#cite_note-353">[350]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-354"><a href="#cite_note-354">[351]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-06decUNAIDS_355-0"><a href="#cite_note-06decUNAIDS-355">[352]</a></sup> However, the National AIDS Programme Myanmar found that 32% of sex workers and 43% of intravenous drug users in Myanmar have HIV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-06decUNAIDS_355-1"><a href="#cite_note-06decUNAIDS-355">[352]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Myanmar" title="Education in Myanmar">Education in Myanmar</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Students,_Hakha,_Chin_State,_Myanmar.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Students%2C_Hakha%2C_Chin_State%2C_Myanmar.jpg/220px-Students%2C_Hakha%2C_Chin_State%2C_Myanmar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Students%2C_Hakha%2C_Chin_State%2C_Myanmar.jpg/330px-Students%2C_Hakha%2C_Chin_State%2C_Myanmar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Students%2C_Hakha%2C_Chin_State%2C_Myanmar.jpg/440px-Students%2C_Hakha%2C_Chin_State%2C_Myanmar.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Students,_Hakha,_Chin_State,_Myanmar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Students on their way to school, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kalaymyo" title="Kalaymyo">Kalaymyo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sagaing_Region" title="Sagaing Region">Sagaing Region</a>, Myanmar</div></div></div>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> Institute of Statistics, Myanmar's official <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Literacy_rate" title="Literacy rate">literacy rate</a> as of 2000 was 90%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-356"><a href="#cite_note-356">[353]</a></sup> Historically, Myanmar has had high literacy rates. The educational system of Myanmar is operated by the government agency, the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Education_(Myanmar)" title="Ministry of Education (Myanmar)">Ministry of Education</a>. The education system is based on the United Kingdom's system after nearly a century of British and Christian presences in Myanmar. Nearly all schools are government-operated, but there has been an increase in privately funded English language schools in the early 21st century. Schooling is compulsory until the end of elementary school, approximately about 9 years old, while the compulsory schooling age is 15 or 16 at international level.
</p><p>There are 101 universities, 12 institutes, 9 degree colleges and 24 colleges in Myanmar, a total of 146 higher education institutions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-357"><a href="#cite_note-357">[354]</a></sup> There are 10 technical training schools, 23 nursing training schools, 1 sport academy and 20 midwifery schools. There are four international schools acknowledged by WASC and College Boardâ<a href="/wiki/International_School_Yangon" title="International School Yangon">The International School Yangon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Myanmar_International_School" title="Myanmar International School">Myanmar International School</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yangon_International_School" title="Yangon International School">Yangon International School</a>, and <a href="/wiki/International_School_of_Myanmar" title="International School of Myanmar">International School of Myanmar</a> in Yangon. Myanmar was ranked 127th in the <a href="/wiki/Global_Innovation_Index" title="Global Innovation Index">Global Innovation Index</a> in 2021.Italy was ranked 29th in the <a href="/wiki/Global_Innovation_Index" title="Global Innovation Index">Global Innovation Index</a> in 2021, up from 30th in 2019<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-358"><a href="#cite_note-358">[355]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crime">Crime</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Crime_in_Myanmar" title="Crime in Myanmar">Crime in Myanmar</a></div>
<p>Myanmar had a murder rate of 15.2 per 100,000 population with a total of 8,044 murders in 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNODC_359-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNODC-359">[356]</a></sup> Factors influencing Myanmar's high murder rate include communal violence and armed conflict.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-360"><a href="#cite_note-360">[357]</a></sup> Myanmar is one of the world's most corrupt nations. The 2012 <a href="/wiki/Transparency_International" title="Transparency International">Transparency International</a> <a href="/wiki/Corruption_Perceptions_Index" title="Corruption Perceptions Index">Corruption Perceptions Index</a> ranked the country at number 171, out of 176 countries in total.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-361"><a href="#cite_note-361">[358]</a></sup> Myanmar is the world's second largest producer of <a href="/wiki/Opium" title="Opium">opium</a> after <a href="/wiki/Opium_production_in_Afghanistan" title="Opium production in Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, producing some 25% of the world's opium, and forms part of the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Triangle_(Southeast_Asia)" title="Golden Triangle (Southeast Asia)">Golden Triangle</a>. The opium industry was a monopoly during colonial times and has since been illegally operated by corrupt officials in the Burmese military and rebel fighters,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-362"><a href="#cite_note-362">[359]</a></sup> primarily as the basis for heroin manufacture. Myanmar is the largest producer of methamphetamines in the world, with the majority of <i><a href="/wiki/Ya_ba" title="Ya ba">Ya ba</a></i> found in Thailand produced in Myanmar, particularly in the Golden Triangle and northeastern Shan State, which borders Thailand, Laos and China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-363"><a href="#cite_note-363">[360]</a></sup> Burmese-produced <i>ya ba</i> is typically trafficked to Thailand via Laos, before being transported through the northeastern Thai region of <a href="/wiki/Isan" title="Isan">Isan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-364"><a href="#cite_note-364">[361]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Myanmar" title="Culture of Myanmar">Culture of Myanmar</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Burmese_Ramayana_dance.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="866" decoding="async" height="251" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Burmese_Ramayana_dance.jpg/170px-Burmese_Ramayana_dance.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Burmese_Ramayana_dance.jpg/255px-Burmese_Ramayana_dance.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Burmese_Ramayana_dance.jpg/340px-Burmese_Ramayana_dance.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Burmese_Ramayana_dance.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Burmese <a href="/wiki/Kinnara" title="Kinnara">Kinnayi Kinnaya</a> dance</div></div></div>
<p>A diverse range of indigenous cultures exist in Myanmar, with majority culture primarily Buddhist and <a href="/wiki/Bamar_people" title="Bamar people">Bamar</a>. Bamar culture has been influenced by the cultures of neighbouring countries, manifested in its language, cuisine, music, dance and theatre. The arts, particularly literature, have historically been influenced by the local form of Theravada Buddhism. Considered the national epic of Myanmar, the <i><a href="/wiki/Yama_Zatdaw" title="Yama Zatdaw">Yama Zatdaw</a></i>, an adaptation of India's <i><a href="/wiki/Ramayana" title="Ramayana">Ramayana</a></i>, has been influenced greatly by Thai, Mon, and Indian versions of the play.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-365"><a href="#cite_note-365">[362]</a></sup> Buddhism is practised along with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nat_(spirit)" title="Nat (spirit)">nat worship</a>, which involves elaborate rituals to propitiate one from a pantheon of 37 nats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-366"><a href="#cite_note-366">[363]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-367"><a href="#cite_note-367">[364]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Myanmar_Traditional_novitiation_march.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="951" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Myanmar_Traditional_novitiation_march.JPG/220px-Myanmar_Traditional_novitiation_march.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Myanmar_Traditional_novitiation_march.JPG/330px-Myanmar_Traditional_novitiation_march.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Myanmar_Traditional_novitiation_march.JPG/440px-Myanmar_Traditional_novitiation_march.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Myanmar_Traditional_novitiation_march.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Buddhist <a href="/wiki/Shinbyu" title="Shinbyu">Shinbyu</a> ceremony in <a href="/wiki/Mandalay" title="Mandalay">Mandalay</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In a traditional village, the monastery is the centre of cultural life. Monks are venerated and supported by the lay people. A novitiation ceremony called <a href="/wiki/Shinbyu" title="Shinbyu">shinbyu</a> is the most important <a href="/wiki/Coming_of_age" title="Coming of age">coming of age</a> events for a boy, during which he enters the monastery for a short time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kmc_368-0"><a href="#cite_note-kmc-368">[365]</a></sup> All male children in Buddhist families are encouraged to be a novice (beginner for Buddhism) before the age of twenty and to be a monk after the age of twenty. Girls have ear-piercing ceremonies (<span title="Burmese-language text"><span lang="my">áá¬á¸á</span></span>) at the same time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kmc_368-1"><a href="#cite_note-kmc-368">[365]</a></sup> Burmese culture is most evident in villages where local festivals are held throughout the year, the most important being the <a href="/wiki/Pagoda_festival" title="Pagoda festival">pagoda festival</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Myam-ma_330-1"><a href="#cite_note-Myam-ma-330">[328]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-369"><a href="#cite_note-369">[366]</a></sup> Many villages have a guardian nat, and superstition and taboos are commonplace.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rakhine_Thingyan_2011.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1333" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Rakhine_Thingyan_2011.jpeg/220px-Rakhine_Thingyan_2011.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Rakhine_Thingyan_2011.jpeg/330px-Rakhine_Thingyan_2011.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Rakhine_Thingyan_2011.jpeg/440px-Rakhine_Thingyan_2011.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rakhine_Thingyan_2011.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a href="/wiki/Rakhine_people" title="Rakhine people">Arakan</a> (Rakhine) girl pours water at revellers during the Burmese New Year <a href="/wiki/Thingyan" title="Thingyan">Thingyan</a> Water Festival in Yangon.</div></div></div>
<p>British colonial rule introduced Western elements of culture to Myanmar. Myanmar's education system is modelled after that of the United Kingdom. Colonial architectural influences are most evident in major cities such as Yangon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-370"><a href="#cite_note-370">[367]</a></sup> Many ethnic minorities, particularly the Karen in the southeast and the Kachin and Chin who populate the north and northeast, practice Christianity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-371"><a href="#cite_note-371">[368]</a></sup> According to <i><a href="/wiki/The_World_Factbook" title="The World Factbook">The World Factbook</a></i>, the Burman population is 68% and the ethnic groups constitute 32%. In contrast, the exiled leaders and organisations claim the country is 40% ethnic.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">Cuisine</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Burmese_cuisine" title="Burmese cuisine">Burmese cuisine</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Burmese_cuisine" title="Burmese cuisine">Burmese cuisine</a> is characterised by extensive use of fish products such as <a href="/wiki/Fish_sauce" title="Fish sauce">fish sauce</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ngapi" title="Ngapi">ngapi</a> (fermented seafood) and dried prawn. <a href="/wiki/Mohinga" title="Mohinga">Mohinga</a> is the traditional breakfast dish and is Myanmar's national dish. Seafood is a common ingredient in coastal cities, while meat and poultry are more commonly used in landlocked cities like Mandalay. Freshwater fish and shrimp have been incorporated into inland cooking as a primary source of protein and are used in a variety of ways, fresh, salted whole or filleted, salted and dried, made into a salty paste, or fermented sour and pressed. Burmese cuisine also includes a variety of salads (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A_thoke" title="A thoke">a thoke</a></i>), centred on one major ingredient, ranging from starches like rice, wheat and rice noodles, glass noodles and vermicelli, to potato, ginger, tomato, <a href="/wiki/Kaffir_lime" title="Kaffir lime">kaffir lime</a>, long bean, and <a href="/wiki/Lahpet" title="Lahpet">lahpet</a> (pickled tea leaves).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sport">Sport</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chinlone_Burma_cropped.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2421" data-file-width="2760" decoding="async" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Chinlone_Burma_cropped.jpg/220px-Chinlone_Burma_cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Chinlone_Burma_cropped.jpg/330px-Chinlone_Burma_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Chinlone_Burma_cropped.jpg/440px-Chinlone_Burma_cropped.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chinlone_Burma_cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Men playing chinlone</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Lethwei" title="Lethwei">Lethwei</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bando" title="Bando">Bando</a>, <a href="/wiki/Banshay" title="Banshay">Banshay</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pongyi_thaing" title="Pongyi thaing">Pongyi thaing</a> martial arts and <a href="/wiki/Chinlone" title="Chinlone">chinlone</a> are traditional sports in Myanmar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-372"><a href="#cite_note-372">[369]</a></sup> Football is played all over the country, even in villages, and its <a href="/wiki/Myanmar_national_football_team" title="Myanmar national football team">national team</a> is ruled by the <a href="/wiki/Myanmar_Football_Federation" title="Myanmar Football Federation">Myanmar Football Federation</a>. The <a href="/wiki/2013_Southeast_Asian_Games" title="2013 Southeast Asian Games">2013 Southeast Asian Games</a> took place in Naypyidaw, Yangon, Mandalay and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ngwesaung_Beach" title="Ngwesaung Beach">Ngwesaung Beach</a> in December representing the third occasion that the event has been staged in Myanmar. Myanmar previously hosted the games in <a href="/wiki/1961_Southeast_Asian_Peninsular_Games" title="1961 Southeast Asian Peninsular Games">1961</a> and <a href="/wiki/1969_Southeast_Asian_Peninsular_Games" title="1969 Southeast Asian Peninsular Games">1969</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-373"><a href="#cite_note-373">[370]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art">Art</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Art_of_Myanmar" title="Art of Myanmar">Art of Myanmar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Myanmar_architecture" title="Myanmar architecture">Myanmar architecture</a></div>
<p>Burmese traditional art concepts are popular and respected by the Burmese people and people from abroad. Burmese contemporary art has developed quite rapidly on its own terms. Artists born after the 1980s have had greater chances of art practice outside the country.
</p><p>One of the first to study western art was <a href="/wiki/Ba_Nyan" title="Ba Nyan">Ba Nyan</a>. Together with <a href="/wiki/Ngwe_Gaing" title="Ngwe Gaing">Ngwe Gaing</a> and a handful of other artists, they were the pioneers of western painting style. Later on most young children learned the concepts from them. Some well known contemporary artists are <a href="/wiki/Lun_Gywe" title="Lun Gywe">Lun Gywe</a>, Aung Kyaw Htet, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MPP_Yei_Myint" title="MPP Yei Myint">MPP Yei Myint</a>, Myint Swe, Min Wai Aung, <a href="/wiki/Aung_Myint" title="Aung Myint">Aung Myint</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kin_Maung_Yin" title="Kin Maung Yin">Kin Maung Yin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Po_Po" title="Po Po">Po Po</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zaw_Zaw_Aung" title="Zaw Zaw Aung">Zaw Zaw Aung</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media_and_communications">Media and communications</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Media_of_Myanmar" title="Media of Myanmar">Media of Myanmar</a></div>
<p>Because of Myanmar's political climate, there are not many media companies in relation to the country's population. Some are privately owned. All programming must meet with the approval of the censorship board. The Burmese government announced on 20 August 2012 that it would stop censoring media before publication. Following the announcement, newspapers and other outlets no longer required approved by state censors; however, journalists in the country can still face consequences for what they write and say.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-374"><a href="#cite_note-374">[371]</a></sup> In April 2013, international media reports were published to relay the enactment of the media liberalisation reforms that we announced in August 2012. For the first time in numerous decades, the publication of privately owned newspapers commenced in the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-375"><a href="#cite_note-375">[372]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Internet">Internet</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Internet_in_Myanmar" title="Internet in Myanmar">Internet in Myanmar</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kayan_women_Burma_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Kayan_women_Burma_1.jpg/220px-Kayan_women_Burma_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Kayan_women_Burma_1.jpg/330px-Kayan_women_Burma_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Kayan_women_Burma_1.jpg/440px-Kayan_women_Burma_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kayan_women_Burma_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kayan_people_(Burma)" title="Kayan people (Burma)">Kayan</a> women in a village near <a href="/wiki/Inle_Lake" title="Inle Lake">Inle Lake</a>, 2010</div></div></div>
<p>Internet use is estimated to be relatively low compared to other countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calderaro_376-0"><a href="#cite_note-Calderaro-376">[373]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-377"><a href="#cite_note-377">[374]</a></sup> Myanmar's internet used to be subject to censorship, and authorities viewed e-mails and posts on Internet blogs until 2012 when the government removed media censorship. During the strict censorship days, activity at internet cafes was regulated, and one blogger named <a href="/wiki/Zarganar" title="Zarganar">Zarganar</a> was sentenced to prison for publishing a video of destruction caused by <a href="/wiki/Cyclone_Nargis" title="Cyclone Nargis">Cyclone Nargis</a> in 2008; Zarganar was released in October 2011.
</p><p>In regards to communications infrastructure, Myanmar is the last ranked Asian country in the World Economic Forum's <a href="/wiki/Networked_Readiness_Index" title="Networked Readiness Index">Networked Readiness Index</a> (NRI) â an indicator for determining the development level of a country's information and communication technologies. With 139 countries reported on, Myanmar ranked number 133 overall in the 2016 NRI ranking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-378"><a href="#cite_note-378">[375]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Film">Film</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Myanmar" title="Cinema of Myanmar">Cinema of Myanmar</a></div>
<p>Myanmar's first film was a documentary of the funeral of Tun Sheinâa leading politician of the 1910s, who campaigned for Burmese independence in London. The first Burmese <a href="/wiki/Silent_film" title="Silent film">silent film</a> <i>Myitta Ne Thuya</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Love_and_Liquor" title="Love and Liquor">Love and Liquor</a></i>) in 1920 which proved a major success, despite its poor quality due to a fixed camera position and inadequate film accessories. During the 1920s and 1930s, many Burmese-owned film companies made and produced several films. The first Burmese <a href="/wiki/Sound_film" title="Sound film">sound film</a> was produced in 1932 in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bombay" title="Bombay">Bombay</a>, India with the title Ngwe Pay Lo Ma Ya (Money Can't Buy It). After World War II, Burmese cinema continued to address political themes. Many of the films produced in the early Cold War era had a strong propaganda element to them.
</p><p>In the era that followed the political events of 1988, the film industry has been increasingly controlled by the government. Film stars who had been involved in the political activities were banned from appearing in films. The government issues strict rules on censorship and largely determines who produces films, as well as who gets academy awards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-379"><a href="#cite_note-379">[376]</a></sup>
</p><p>Over the years, the movie industry has also shifted to producing many lower-budget <a href="/wiki/Direct-to-video" title="Direct-to-video">direct-to-video</a> films. Most of the movies produced nowadays are <a href="/wiki/Comedy_film" title="Comedy film">comedies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-380"><a href="#cite_note-380">[377]</a></sup> In 2008, only 12 films worthy of being considered for an <a href="/wiki/Academy_Awards" title="Academy Awards">Academy Award</a> were made, although at least 800 VCDs were produced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-381"><a href="#cite_note-381">[378]</a></sup> Myanmar is the primary subject of a 2007 graphic novel titled <i>Chroniques Birmanes</i> by <a href="/wiki/Quebec_City" title="Quebec City">QuÃ©bÃ©cois</a> author and animator, <a href="/wiki/Guy_Delisle" title="Guy Delisle">Guy Delisle</a>. The graphic novel was translated into English under the title <i><a href="/wiki/Burma_Chronicles" title="Burma Chronicles">Burma Chronicles</a></i> in 2008. In 2009, a documentary about Burmese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Videojournalist" title="Videojournalist">videojournalists</a> called <i><a href="/wiki/Burma_VJ" title="Burma VJ">Burma VJ</a></i> was released.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-382"><a href="#cite_note-382">[379]</a></sup> This film was nominated for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Documentary_Feature" title="Academy Award for Best Documentary Feature">Best Documentary Feature</a> at the <a href="/wiki/82nd_Academy_Awards" title="82nd Academy Awards">2010 Academy Awards</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-383"><a href="#cite_note-383">[380]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lady_(2011_film)" title="The Lady (2011 film)">The Lady</a></i> had its world premiere on 12 September 2011 at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/36th_Toronto_International_Film_Festival" title="36th Toronto International Film Festival">36th Toronto International Film Festival</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-384"><a href="#cite_note-384">[381]</a></sup>
</p>

Note: Wikipedia's IPA conventions require indicating /r/ even in British English although only some British English speakers pronounce r at the end of syllables. As <a href="/wiki/John_C._Wells" title="John C. Wells">John Wells</a> explains, the English spellings of both Myanmar and Burma assume a non-rhotic variety of English, in which the letter r before a consonant or finally serves merely to indicate a long vowel: [ËmjÃ¦nmÉË, ËbÉËmÉ]. So the pronunciation of the last syllable of Myanmar as [mÉËr] or of Burma as [bÉËrmÉ] by some speakers in the UK and most speakers in North America is in fact a <a href="/wiki/Spelling_pronunciation" title="Spelling pronunciation">spelling pronunciation</a> based on a misunderstanding of non-rhotic spelling conventions. The final <i>r</i> in <i>Myanmar</i> was not intended for pronunciation and is there to ensure that the final a is pronounced with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Broad_a" title="Broad a">broad <i>ah</i></a> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ÉË/: 'a' in 'father'">ÉË</span></span>/</a></span></span>) in "father". If the Burmese name <span title="Burmese-language text"><span lang="my">áá¼ááºáá¬</span></span> <small></small><span class="IPA" lang="my-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Burmese" title="Help:IPA/Burmese">[mjÉmÃ ]</a></span> were spelled "Myanma" in English, this would be pronounced <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/É/: 'a' in 'about'">É</span></span>/</a></span></span> at the end by all English speakers. If it were spelled "Myanmah", the end would be pronounced <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ÉË/: 'a' in 'father'">ÉË</span></span>/</a></span></span> by all English speakers. <span style="font-size:85%;">See other English pronunciations in <a href="#Etymology">Â§ Etymology</a></span>.</span>
</li>
